{"uri": "8176274974", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-13", "time": "15:09:56", "dateTime": "2024-06-13T15:09:56Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-13T15:08:47Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9803921580314636, "url": "https://www.medpagetoday.com/gastroenterology/livertransplantation/110623", "title": "98-Year-Old Man Believed to Be the Oldest American Organ Donor Ever", "body": "ST. LOUIS -- Orville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff, Missouri, on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to Saint Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature -- always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor -- the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. He was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing (UNOS), a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased -- more than 16,000 -- and for the number of organ transplants performed -- more than 46,000 -- according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past 5 years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois, and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first 4 months of this year were people ages 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" David Klassen, MD, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he added. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren, and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" he said.", "source": {"uri": "medpagetoday.com", "dataType": {"uri": "8175728607", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-13", "time": "09:35:30", "dateTime": "2024-06-13T09:35:30Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-13T09:33:03Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9686274528503418, "url": "https://www.newstribune.com/news/2024/jun/13/98-year-old-man-believed-to-be-oldest-american/", "title": "98-year-old man believed to be oldest American organ donor", "body": "ST. LOUIS (AP) -- Orville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall May 27 while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff, Missouri, said his daughter, Linda Mitchelle. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St. Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature -- always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor -- the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. He was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or UNOS, a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased -- more than 16,000 -- and for the number of organ transplants performed -- more than 46,000 -- according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12 percent of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register. In fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\nThis undated photo provided by Linda Mitchelle shows Orville Allen of Poplar Bluff, Mo., holding his great-granddaughter at her baptism. Allen died Wednesday, May 29, 2024, and his liver was successfully donated and transplanted to a 72-year-old woman. Transplant organizations say Allen is the oldest American ever to donate an organ upon death. {"uri": "8175930669", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-13", "time": "11:35:38", "dateTime": "2024-06-13T11:35:38Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-13T11:34:52Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9607843160629272, "url": "https://journalnow.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/a-98-year-old-missouri-man-s-liver-was-donated-he-may-be-the-oldest/article_a60a73a5-5387-5cf5-aed1-d704a31b7d36.html", "title": "A 98-year-old Missouri man's liver was donated. He may be the oldest US organ donor ever.", "body": "ST. LOUIS -- Orville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St. Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature -- always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor -- the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. Lockhart was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or UNOS, a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased -- more than 16,000 -- and for the number of organ transplants performed -- more than 46,000 -- according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\nLocations Stlouis Tags Lee-national Wire Dcc {"uri": "2024-06-387615792", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-12", "time": "20:29:19", "dateTime": "2024-06-12T20:29:19Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-12T20:26:36Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9333333373069763, "url": "https://indianexpress.com/article/world/98-year-old-mans-liver-was-donated-he-is-believed-to-be-oldest-american-organ-donor-9389008/", "title": "A 98-year-old man's liver was donated. He is believed to be the oldest American organ donor ever | World News - The Indian Express", "body": "Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney.\n\nOrville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said.\n\nHe died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff, Missouri, on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St. Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature -- always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor -- the siblings didn't hesitate.\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. He was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or UNOS, a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades.\n\nLast year was a record year for donations from the deceased more than 16,000 -- and for the number of organ transplants performed more than 46,000 according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages.\n\nEarlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.The liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said.\n\nIn fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors. Allen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War.\n\nAfter the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel. He also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades.\n\nHe and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.", "source": {"uri": "indianexpress.com", "dataType": He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted into a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nHe was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle, said.\n\nHe struck the back of his head and was flown to St Francis Medical Centre in Cape Girardeau.\n\nGiven Mr Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their father's nature - always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a neighbour in need - the siblings did not hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Ms Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart, of West Virginia, was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Centre for Organ Recovery and Education, which co-ordinated recovery of his liver. He was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nLast year was a record year for donations from the deceased - more than 16,000 - and for the number of organ transplants performed (more than 46,000), according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nMr Allen was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in the Second World War, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? {"uri": "8174644667", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-12", "time": "16:57:12", "dateTime": "2024-06-12T16:57:12Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-12T16:49:45Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9176470637321472, "url": "https://www.dailymail.co.uk/wires/ap/article-13523055/A-98-year-old-mans-liver-donated-He-believed-oldest-American-organ-donor-ever.html", "title": "A 98-year-old man's liver was donated. He is believed to be the...", "body": "ST. LOUIS (AP) - Orville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff, Missouri, on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St. Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature - always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor - the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\nThis photo provided by Linda Mitchelle shows Orville Allen of Poplar Bluff, Mo., hugging his great-grandson in March 2024. Allen died Wednesday, May 29, 2024, and his liver was successfully donated and transplanted to a 72-year-old woman. Transplant organizations say Allen is the oldest American ever to donate an organ upon death. (Linda Mitchelle via AP)\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. He was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or UNOS, a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased - more than 16,000 - and for the number of organ transplants performed - more than 46,000 - according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation\u00b4s list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\n\"As we\u00b4ve seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won\u00b4t consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I\u00b4m going to put donor on my driver\u00b4s license right now,\u00b4\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\nThis undated photo provided by Linda Mitchelle shows Orville Allen of Poplar Bluff, Mo., holding his great-granddaughter at her baptism. Allen died Wednesday, May 29, 2024, and his liver was successfully donated and transplanted to a 72-year-old woman. Transplant organizations say Allen is the oldest American ever to donate an organ upon death. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\nLocations Stlouis Tags Lee-national Wire {"uri": "8175953813", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-13", "time": "11:50:07", "dateTime": "2024-06-13T11:50:07Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-13T11:49:14Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9098039269447327, "url": "https://pressofatlanticcity.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/a-98-year-old-missouri-man-s-liver-was-donated-he-may-be-the-oldest/article_9ea57612-38df-54ee-9040-c22b58fb2163.html", "title": "A 98-year-old Missouri man's liver was donated. He may be the oldest US organ donor ever.", "body": "ST. LOUIS -- Orville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St. Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nPeople are also reading... Shirley MacLaine, 90, is filming a new movie in Atlantic City's iconic Ducktown neighborhood Pedestrian struck, killed on Route 30 in Absecon 7.6-pound summer flounder caught in Atlantic City's Gardner's Basin: Shep On Fishing Mays Landing Red Lobster set to close as part of company's bankruptcy Atlantic Cape graduate opens new coffee bar in Smithville Mainland Regional Board of Education hires new AD Egg Harbor Township man seriously injured in Fire Road crash How much time is J.T. Realmuto expected to miss after knee surgery? Mays Landing woman indicted in death of Stockton University student Playland building on Ocean City Boardwalk is up but not open three years after fire $18.5M Aerospace Innovation Center coming to Egg Harbor Township tech park, Murphy says Somers Point announces lineup for 2024 AtlantiCare Concerts on the Beach Stockton experiment changes how Atlantic City, Pleasantville police conduct traffic stops Galloway man dies after being struck by two vehicles while riding electric scooter Ocean City police charge 6 teens with vehicle theft\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature -- always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor -- the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. Lockhart was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or UNOS, a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased -- more than 16,000 -- and for the number of organ transplants performed -- more than 46,000 -- according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\nLocations Stlouis Tags Lee-national Wire Dcc {"uri": "8175942341", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-13", "time": "11:42:32", "dateTime": "2024-06-13T11:42:32Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-13T11:41:50Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9098039269447327, "url": "https://magicvalley.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/a-98-year-old-missouri-man-s-liver-was-donated-he-may-be-the-oldest/article_763ea5bd-c0ba-5a57-8e97-f7981eb29375.html", "title": "A 98-year-old Missouri man's liver was donated. He may be the oldest US organ donor ever.", "body": "ST. LOUIS -- Orville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St. Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nPeople are also reading... In-N-Out Burger proposes Twin Falls location Twin Falls celebrates Pride Festival Death Notices World War II veteran marries his bride near Normandy's D-Day beaches. He's 100, she's 96 Passenger dies in 3-vehicle crash on I-84 near Burley Firefighters respond to pair of blazes in Snake River Canyon Death Notices BROTHERLY LOVE: Buhl's Brunson twins sign with DIII wrestling power Wartburg College Former state senator announces candidacy for GOP chair, will challenge Dorothy Moon 2 arrested on drug charges in Jerome County Reported birth of rare white buffalo calf in Yellowstone park fulfills Lakota prophecy Five rodeo state champions return to defend titles Kimberly man found guilty of 10 charges of lewd conduct Blazing ball determined cause of canyon fire Death Notices\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature -- always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor -- the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. Lockhart was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or UNOS, a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased -- more than 16,000 -- and for the number of organ transplants performed -- more than 46,000 -- according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\n0 Comments Love 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted into a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nHe was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle, said.\n\nHe struck the back of his head and was flown to St Francis Medical Centre in Cape Girardeau.\n\nIt turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life Linda Mitchelle, Orville Allen's daughter\n\nSwelling around Mr Allen's brain could not be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nGiven Mr Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their father's nature - always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a neighbour in need - the siblings did not hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Ms Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart, of West Virginia, was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Centre for Organ Recovery and Education, which co-ordinated recovery of his liver. He was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nOrville Allen holding his great-granddaughter at her baptism (Linda Mitchelle via AP)\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing (UNOS), a non-profit organisation that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades.\n\nLast year was a record year for donations from the deceased - more than 16,000 - and for the number of organ transplants performed (more than 46,000), according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nMr Allen was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in the Second World War, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nTo me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family Greg Allen, one of Orville Allen's sons\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's licence right now',\" Ms Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Mr Allen's sons, said the ability to donate their father's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad {"uri": "8174962558", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-12", "time": "21:32:00", "dateTime": "2024-06-12T21:32:00Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-12T21:31:16Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9098039269447327, "url": "https://www.theguardian.com/us-news/article/2024/jun/12/oldest-american-organ-donor-orville-allen", "title": "Oldest ever US organ donor believed to be 98-year-old man", "body": "Orville Allen, second world war and Korean war veteran, died on 29 May and his liver was successfully transplanted\n\nOrville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both second world war and the Korean war and a longtime educator in rural south-eastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff, Missouri, on 27 May, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St Francis medical center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: would they consider donating his liver?\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature - always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor - the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. He was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or Unos, a non-profit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased - more than 16,000 - and for the number of organ transplants performed - more than 46,000 - according to Unos. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and north-eastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, Unos spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr David Klassen, chief medical officer for Unos, said in a statement. But the impact of ageing varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of south-east Missouri. He was a pilot in the army air corps in the second world war, then served in artillery communications in the army 1st cavalry division in the Korean war. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the army reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville high school, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.", "source": {"uri": "theguardian.com", The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\nLocations Stlouis Tags Lee-national Wire Dcc He may be the oldest US organ donor ever.", "body": "ST. LOUIS -- Orville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St. Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nPeople are also reading... Deere & Co. to pay $1.1 million in back wages, interest to Black and Hispanic applicants Hy-Vee holds event at closing Logan Avenue store, customers still upset Proposed Cedar Falls housing development has neighbors concerned 3 Iowa U.S. House members seek $115M in earmarks UPDATE: Deere posts notice of 123 layoffs, 49 in Waterloo; global production, salaried employees cut 2024 U.S. Open long shot picks: Odds and dark horse picks for U.S. Open golf at Pinehurst College Men's Basketball: Highly-touted forward commits to UNI in class of 2025 Child injured in Cedar Falls accident My Waterloo Days brings diverse community together in one block 'The Vibe' takes over Scoopski's location, still offering ice cream One arrested in overnight Waterloo shooting that sent man to hospital Two injured in three shootings Names released in fatal motorcycle crash Waterloo native takes reins at Oklahoma City school district Man arrested for stolen car found crashed\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature -- always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor -- the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. Lockhart was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or UNOS, a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased -- more than 16,000 -- and for the number of organ transplants performed -- more than 46,000 -- according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\n0 Comments Love 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry {"uri": "8175944762", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-13", "time": "11:44:28", "dateTime": "2024-06-13T11:44:28Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-13T11:43:17Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9058823585510254, "url": "https://tucson.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/a-98-year-old-missouri-man-s-liver-was-donated-he-may-be-the-oldest/article_444e86a2-30cc-54b0-815f-b8ad03550a60.html", "title": "A 98-year-old Missouri man's liver was donated. He may be the oldest US organ donor ever.", "body": "ST. LOUIS -- Orville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St. Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nPeople are also reading... A water supply used for Arizona's suburban growth 'is gone,' one negotiator says Tucson's former Foothills Mall site getting new hotel Ube, creme br\u00fbl\u00e9e, horchata: Cal's Bakeshop is taking over Tucson one doughnut at a time \ud83c\udf69 American tourist electrocuted, and another injured, in Rocky Point hot tub New homes carved out of historic downtown Tucson hotel Arizona treasurer tells AG to let others investigate Hobbs administration contract Tucson icon Linda Ronstadt regains ability to speak after bout with COVID Judge suspends Kari Lake attorney for lying to Supreme Court What will monsoon look like this year? Here are the 2024 predictions \u26c8 World War II veteran marries his bride near Normandy's D-Day beaches. He's 100, she's 96 Converted student housing complex in central Tucson sells for $13.6M U of A names John Arnold to $550,000 permanent executive position This new curated shop will also serve coffee and pastries from local bakers Football recruiting: Arizona's QB commitments, ASU and UCLA stay hot and USC preps for visits Appeals Court says Kari Lake isn't entitled to rerun of election she lost\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature -- always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor -- the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. Lockhart was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or UNOS, a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased -- more than 16,000 -- and for the number of organ transplants performed -- more than 46,000 -- according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\nLocations Stlouis Tags Lee-national Wire Dcc\n\nSubscribe to stay connected to Tucson. {"uri": "8175952275", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-13", "time": "11:49:21", "dateTime": "2024-06-13T11:49:21Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-13T11:48:17Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8941176533699036, "url": "https://siouxcityjournal.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/a-98-year-old-missouri-man-s-liver-was-donated-he-may-be-the-oldest/article_fb7c26bb-1aec-5228-bc1a-a159c8571149.html", "title": "A 98-year-old Missouri man's liver was donated. He may be the oldest US organ donor ever.", "body": "ST. LOUIS -- Orville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St. Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nPeople are also reading... Former Sioux City hotel under renovation, to become high-rise senior housing this year 2 charged in stomping man downtown Man charged with stabbing at Sioux City hotel Council moves forward street vacations for Sioux Gateway hotel project Top area softball performers: MOC-FV 8th grader, East, Le Mars, West Sioux juniors shine Marriage licenses for Woodbury County for June 10, 2024 Journal Special Report: Heating issue at new Woodbury County jail caused months of contentious back-and-forth Northwestern All-American QB Gramstad tries out at Husker post-grad camp Le Mars man arrested after standoff for breaking woman's nose Elk Creek Road construction project begins next week NW Iowa softball top performers: Five individuals, five teams that stand out Woodbury County Court Report for June 11, 2024 Sioux City Marina operator countersues city over lease dispute FLASHBACK: Craftsman: Judd Nelson plies intensity for his work Behold the footlong 'Bratzilla,' new to the Sioux City Explorers' concession stand lineup\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature -- always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor -- the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. Lockhart was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or UNOS, a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased -- more than 16,000 -- and for the number of organ transplants performed -- more than 46,000 -- according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\n0 Comments Love 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry
