{"uri": "8175954901", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-13", "time": "11:50:50", "dateTime": "2024-06-13T11:50:50Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-13T11:49:56Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8823529481887817, "url": "https://statesville.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/a-98-year-old-missouri-man-s-liver-was-donated-he-may-be-the-oldest/article_a8747d4a-49bf-52d5-8565-94eb9e5727c4.html", "title": "A 98-year-old Missouri man's liver was donated. He may be the oldest US organ donor ever.", "body": "ST. LOUIS -- Orville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St. Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nPeople are also reading... Iredell-Statesville Schools accepts $143 million bid for high school in Troutman Statesville woman found dead on Hickory Avenue; death considered homicide Iredell shelter dog once out of options now has a job, new lease on life Plane makes emergency landing near Midway Road; no injuries reported 'River Monsters' TV host Jeremy Wade kicks off Grandfather Presents speaker series June 27 UNC researchers launch study of thyroid cancer incidence in North Carolina Statesville DIY Skatepark more than a place to skate 67-year-old NC woman killed when she met man for online marketplace transaction Mooresville police arrest 15 in child predator sting operation Pets of the Week: Sweet older dog looking for home to call her own Iredell Health System welcomes familiar face as new director of Iredell Health Foundation ALL-COUNTY SOFTBALL: Mooresville's Crum, Schaen share county's top honor GIRLS SOCCER: 4 from Iredell make All-State Statesville Police Department announces creation of South Statesville Junior Police Ambassadors Iredell Health honors long-serving employees: Marsha Tomlin never wavered from desire to be a nurse\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature -- always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor -- the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. Lockhart was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or UNOS, a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased -- more than 16,000 -- and for the number of organ transplants performed -- more than 46,000 -- according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\nLocations Stlouis Tags Lee-national Wire Dcc He may be the oldest US organ donor ever.", "body": "ST. LOUIS -- Orville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff, Missouri, on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St. Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nAfter smallest Saturday crowd in decades, Cardinals attendance lows piling up Missouri teacher 'wouldn't recommend' OnlyFans career, says she's a 'total outcast' Letter: Trump was counting on a MAGA juror who didn't show up Kansas City home built by Whitey Herzog is for sale, complete with bat banister Spirit of St. Louis air show to feature Boeing F-15 demo, Blue Angels and more St. Louis Public Schools asks teachers to drive buses this fall Battlehawks slip in attendance, TV ratings but still finish atop UFL: Media Views Teen driver gets 4 months in deaths of 3 Lincoln County teens. Families outraged. Mothers allege Kirkwood Schools withheld yearbook sex survey records Hochman: Battlehawk down. Embarrassing play leads to season's end in home playoff game. Francis Howell schools scrap video platform over claims it pushes 'social agendas' McClellan: Today's politics are a slow-motion trainwreck Fallout continues for wealthy St. Louis couple accused of poisoning neighbor's trees BenFred: There goes the Cardinals' momentum again. Here's why it keeps happening. Renovated Kirkwood home includes rentable outdoor oasis\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature -- always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor -- the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. He was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or UNOS, a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased -- more than 16,000 -- and for the number of organ transplants performed -- more than 46,000 -- according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\n0 Comments", "source": {"uri": He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\n0 Comments Love 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry He may be the oldest US organ donor ever.", "body": "ST. LOUIS -- Orville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St. Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nPeople are also reading... 10 suburban Catholic churches in Erie, Niagara counties slated to be closed by Buffalo Diocese Buffalo Catholics stunned, distressed by plan to close 14 churches Angola man arrested after crash that killed 8-year-old boy in Hamburg Buffalo woman celebrates 110th birthday, becomes a supercentenarian 15 Buffalo parishes to merge with other churches as diocesan restructuring begins Sabres GM for a Day: Patrick Kane headlines series of bold offseason moves Another day, another round of heartache for area Catholics as more churches learn their fate Seven more Catholic church closures recommended, this time for northern Niagara and Orleans counties Anger and sadness at Sunday Mass as news of church mergers and closings sinks in Buffalo Bills unveil parking changes for 2024 season 'To know him was to love him': Loved ones mourn 8-year-old killed in Hamburg crash Erie County real estate transactions West Seneca workers performed flood-control work without permits Diocese recommends closing 14 churches in western part of Southern Tier Transgender woman sues Buffalo City Mission: Staff 'hid' her in chapel for protection at shelter\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature -- always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor -- the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. Lockhart was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or UNOS, a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased -- more than 16,000 -- and for the number of organ transplants performed -- more than 46,000 -- according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\n0 Comments Locations Stlouis Tags {"uri": "8175936503", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-13", "time": "11:39:23", "dateTime": "2024-06-13T11:39:23Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-13T11:38:18Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8627451062202454, "url": "https://napavalleyregister.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/a-98-year-old-missouri-man-s-liver-was-donated-he-may-be-the-oldest/article_6e5461c1-94b3-5c06-9a71-f58dfbfde7f6.html", "title": "A 98-year-old Missouri man's liver was donated. He may be the oldest US organ donor ever.", "body": "ST. LOUIS -- Orville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St. Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nPeople are also reading... Update: Man shot and killed at Lake Berryessa park identified; another man detained Napa Valley tasting room faces $1,800 fine Update: Man sought after Napa County hit-and-run crash that killed one, injured five Missing motorcyclist found dead off of Mount Veeder Road Update: Man accused of murder, assault after Lake Berryessa shooting Police: Woman arrested in Napa on DUI, child endangerment allegations Warren Winiarski, Napa winemaker who scored 1976 'Judgment of Paris' win, dead at 95 Napa art supply store Color Theory to close 'Still Blake' -- Napa teen back at home after spinal cord injury Finishing touches underway for Biscuits BBQ in downtown Napa Napa's Valley Oak graduates talk of overcoming odds, remember Sasha Mufich Death of cyclists in Napa leads to criminal and civil lawsuits, documentary Police: Man arrested in Vallejo after biting ex-girlfriend, stealing watch in Napa World War II veteran marries his bride near Normandy's D-Day beaches. He's 100, she's 96 Napa County gives raise to its employees\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature -- always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor -- the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. Lockhart was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or UNOS, a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased -- more than 16,000 -- and for the number of organ transplants performed -- more than 46,000 -- according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\nLocations Stlouis Tags Lee-national Wire Dcc {"uri": "8175934676", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-13", "time": "11:38:19", "dateTime": "2024-06-13T11:38:19Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-13T11:37:19Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8588235378265381, "url": "https://mcdowellnews.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/a-98-year-old-missouri-man-s-liver-was-donated-he-may-be-the-oldest/article_9929f93b-e168-5832-ab01-a5ba0ca19869.html", "title": "A 98-year-old Missouri man's liver was donated. He may be the oldest US organ donor ever.", "body": "ST. LOUIS -- Orville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St. Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nPeople are also reading... Marion's Pizza Joint under new ownership; food truck Voodoo Roux is next door McDowell County woman wins $1 million on scratch-off ticket McDowell High School graduates 320 students Emergency personnel searching for missing man in Marion McDowell High graduation: Shuttle service for parking, no limit on attendance Black bear is unwelcome and rude visitor to McDowell home After 32 years, nurse at Mission Hospital McDowell retires Column: Success in women's sports at MHS far-reaching this school year Remembering Robert Boggs: McDowell community college president wore many hats McDowell resident asks county to consider school safety; leaders to approve budget this month North Carolina man extinguishes flaming neighbor after explosion Update: Emergency personnel locate missing disabled man McDowell County leaders to hold hearing on budget; hear update on former Clinchfield Mill building McDowell Virtual Academy graduates 34 students Mission Health awards 23 health career scholarships, 4 to McDowell students\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature -- always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor -- the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. Lockhart was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or UNOS, a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased -- more than 16,000 -- and for the number of organ transplants performed -- more than 46,000 -- according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\nLocations Stlouis Tags Lee-national Wire Dcc LOUIS -- Orville Allen lived a lifetime of service, and when he died at age 98 he had one last thing to give: his liver.\n\nAllen, a veteran of both World War II and the Korean War and a longtime educator in rural southeastern Missouri, is the oldest American to ever donate an organ, transplant organizations said. He died on May 29 and his liver was successfully transplanted to a 72-year-old woman, according to Mid-America Transplant.\n\nAllen was in robust health until he suffered a fall while picking up storm debris at his home in Poplar Bluff on May 27, his daughter, Linda Mitchelle said. He struck the back of his head and was flown to St. Francis Medical Center in Cape Girardeau.\n\nSwelling around Allen's brain couldn't be healed. As the family was preparing to say goodbye, hospital staff had a question: Would they consider donating his liver?\n\nPeople are also reading... Motorcyclist killed in Tuesday morning crash in Bloomington Bloomington buys Owen Nursery property for new public works campus Bloomington man detained on seven drug-related charges in McLean County Over 55,000 expected for Tailgate N' Tallboys music fest in Bloomington State: Murder defendant fought Bloomington man over a gun before fatal shooting Bloomington man sentenced to 16 years after police say he aimed gun at officers 1 man injured in weekend shooting in Bloomington Missing 78-year-old man found safe in Normal Illinois State Police shut down I-55 for blowing dust, 'zero visibility' Students awarded over $80K at Joint Scholarship Celebration in Bloomington Flick Fact: What's so special about this south Bloomington locale? McLean County proposes referendum to eliminate auditor's office Murder trial in 2022 shooting death begins in Bloomington Lifelong Access announces plans to grow during Bloomington fundraiser featuring Doug Collins President Joe Biden's son Hunter Biden is convicted of all 3 felony charges in federal gun trial\n\nGiven Allen's age, it was a question that caught the relatives by surprise. But surgeons had examined him and determined the organ was acceptable for transplant.\n\nKnowing their dad's nature -- always the first to check in on people, always at the doorstep of a needy neighbor -- the siblings didn't hesitate.\n\n\"It turned it from being such a sad loss of our dad to having this little ray of joy because he was doing what he'd done all his life,\" Mitchelle said. \"He was giving one more gift.\"\n\nPreviously, Cecil Lockhart of West Virginia was the oldest person to donate an organ upon death, according to the Center for Organ Recovery & Education, which coordinated recovery of his liver. Lockhart was 95 when he died in 2021, and his liver was successfully transplanted to a woman.\n\nMore people than ever are getting new organs, according to data from the United Network for Organ Sharing, or UNOS, a nonprofit organization that has run the transplant system under a government contract for nearly four decades. Last year was a record year for donations from the deceased -- more than 16,000 -- and for the number of organ transplants performed -- more than 46,000 -- according to UNOS. Liver transplants topped 10,000 for the first time ever.\n\nStill, more than 100,000 people are on the nation's list for a new organ and many will die waiting. The need is so great that scientists are working on alternatives to ease the ongoing shortages. Earlier this year in Massachusetts, Richard \"Rick\" Slayman became the first recipient of a genetically modified pig kidney. He died two months after the transplant.\n\nIncreasingly, older adults can donate organs upon their death, said Kevin Lee, president and CEO of Mid-America Transplant.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"As we've seen advances in medical science, we have been educating over the past five years nurses and hospital staff not to think about age when they call in those referrals, but really allow our medical team and transplant physicians to evaluate the medical eligibility of each individual,\" Lee said.\n\nTwo years ago, a liver was procured from a 90-year-old donor in Mid-America's region, which includes eastern Missouri, southern Illinois and northeastern Arkansas, Lee said. Last fall, livers were donated by an 88-year-old and an 84-year-old.\n\nThe liver \"is resilient. We see liver donations at all ages,\" Lee said.\n\nAbout 12% of deceased organ donors in the first four months of this year were people age 65 or older, UNOS spokeswoman Anne Paschke said.\n\n\"Organ donation at advanced ages can be successful and provide life-saving benefits for the recipients,\" Dr. David Klassen, chief medical officer for UNOS, said in a statement. But the impact of aging varies by organ, he said. In fact, many transplant centers won't consider hearts from senior donors.\n\nAllen was a lifelong resident of southeast Missouri. He was a pilot in the Army Air Corps in World War II, then served in artillery communications in the Army 1st Cavalry Division in the Korean War. After the wars, he spent 27 years in the Army Reserve, retiring as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nHe also farmed and taught vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School, near Poplar Bluff, for nearly four decades. He and his wife of 70 years, Geraldine, who died in 2019, had three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.\n\nHe never got around to signing up to be an organ donor, but the family said they hope his story spurs more people to register.\n\nIn fact, they said, it already has.\n\n\"A whole bunch of people at the visitation and funeral who were former students and friends said, 'You know what? I'm going to put donor on my driver's license right now,'\" Mitchelle said.\n\nGreg Allen, one of Orville's sons, said the ability to donate their dad's organ was uplifting in an otherwise sad time.\n\n\"To me, it's just a wonderful thing to be able to help somebody else, anybody else, to extend their life for their family,\" Greg Allen said.\n\nLove 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry Right: Allen with his youngest great-granddaughter at her baptism.\n\nA 98-year-old war veteran from Missouri has become the oldest organ donor ever in the U.S.\n\nAfter a life of service, Orville Duane Allen and his family made the final act to save someone's life by donating his liver.\n\n\"Our dad was beloved by his family, his students, and the soldiers he led because he was such a caring man who brought out the best in others,\" said Allen's daughter Linda Mitchelle, in a press release.\n\n\"We are grateful to Mid-America Transplant and St. Francis Medical Center for the opportunity to continue his impact through organ donation - a decision I know he would have made without hesitation,\" she said.\n\nAllen's liver helped save a 72-year-old woman. The transplant happened in Missouri on May 31.\n\nCEO and president Kevin Lee of Mid-America Transplant, a nonprofit organization based in St. Louis, said the successful procedure is a testament that being an organ donor is helpful at any age.\n\n\"Mr. Allen was truly a hero, and it is an honor and a privilege to be part of his legacy,\" Lee said in a press release. \"We hope his final act of kindness as a donor hero inspires others, regardless of age, to say yes to lifesaving organ and tissue donation.\"\n\nAllen was a veteran of both World War II, where he served as a pilot, and the Korean War.\n\nHe served 27 years in the U.S. Army Reserve, and retired as a lieutenant colonel.\n\nAllen also spent four decades teaching vocational agriculture at Neelyville High School in Missouri, which is his alma mater.\n\nHe was married for 70 years to his late wife Alice Geraldine Allen, with whom he shared three children, three grandchildren and six great-grandchildren.", "source": {"uri":
